s.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/NazliniMudhills.jpg?resize=600%2C600&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn31@2020_5/2021/04/03/04-49-42-230_025634dc8cd3857d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/brainpickings/rss/~4/tyI72a2pNmo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn51@2020_5/2021/04/03/04-49-46-461_45ad2e0a44f20bfe.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1617425326481},{"createdTime":"2021-04-03 12:48:46","updatedTime":"2021-04-03 12:48:46","title":"Art and the Human Spirit: Olivia Laing on What the Lives of Great Artists Reveal About Vulnerability, Love, Loneliness, Resistance, and Our Search for Meaning","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=72814","description":"<h3>&#8220;We’re so often told that art can’t really change anything. But&#8230; it shapes our ethical landscapes; it opens us to the interior lives of others. It is a training ground for possibility. It makes plain inequalities, and it offers other ways of living.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Funny-Weather-Emergency-Olivia-Laing/dp/132400570X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"486\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?fit=320%2C486&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"Art and the Human Spirit: Olivia Laing on What the Lives of Great Artists Reveal About Vulnerability, Love, Loneliness, Resistance, and Our Search for Meaning\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?w=790&#38;ssl=1 790w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?resize=240%2C365&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?resize=320%2C486&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?resize=768%2C1167&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?resize=600%2C911&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>The composite creation of <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/12/14/you-must-change-rilke-rodin-empathy/\">a doctor, a philosopher, a poet, and a sculptor</a>, the word <em>empathy</em> in the modern sense only came into use at the dawn of the twentieth century as a term for the imaginative act of projecting yourself into a work of art, into a world of feeling and experience other than your own. It vesselled in language that peculiar, ineffable way art has of bringing you closer to yourself by taking you out of yourself &#8212; its singular power to furnish, Iris Murdoch&#8217;s exquisite phrasing, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/10/21/iris-murdoch-unselfing/\">&#8220;an occasion for unselfing.&#8221;</a> And yet this notion cinches the central paradox of art: Every artist makes what they make with the whole of who they are &#8212; with the totality of experiences, beliefs, impressions, obsessions, childhood confusions, heartbreaks, inner conflicts, and contradictions that constellate a self. To be an artist is to put this  combinatorial self in the service of furnishing occasions for unselfing in others. </p>\n<p>That may be why the lives of artists have such singular allure as case studies and models of turning the confusion, complexity, and uncertainty of life into something beautiful and lasting &#8212; something that harmonizes the disquietude and dissonance of living.</p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/georgiaokeeffe3.jpg?w=680&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Red Hill and White Shell</em> by Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe, 1938</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Funny-Weather-Emergency-Olivia-Laing/dp/132400570X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=_\"blank\"><strong><em>Funny Weather: Art in an Emergency</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/funny-weather-art-in-an-emergency/oclc/1122876898&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\"><em>public library</em></a>), <strong>Olivia Laing</strong> &#8212; one of the handful of living writers whose mind and prose I enjoy commensurately with the Whitmans and the Woolfs of yore &#8212; occasions a rare gift of unselfing through the lives and worlds of painters, poets, filmmakers, novelists, and musicians who have imprinted culture in a profound way while living largely outside the standards and stabilities of society, embodying of James Baldwin&#8217;s piercing insight that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/08/20/james-baldwin-the-creative-process/\">&#8220;a society must assume that it is stable, but the artist must know, and he must let us know, that there is nothing stable under heaven.&#8221;</a> </p>\n<p>Punctuating these biographical sketches laced with larger questions about art and the human spirit are Laing&#8217;s personal essays reflecting, through the lens of her own lived experience, on existential questions of freedom, desire, loneliness, queerness, democracy, rebellion, abandonment, and the myriad vulnerable tendrils of aliveness that make life worth living. </p>\n<p>What emerges is a case for art as a truly human endeavor, made by human beings with bodies and identities and beliefs often at odds with the collective imperative; art as &#8220;a zone of both enchantment and resistance,&#8221; art as sentinel and witness of &#8220;how truth is made, diagramming the stages of its construction, or as it may be dissolution,&#8221; art as &#8220;a direct response to the paucity and hostility of the culture at large,&#8221; art as a buoy for loneliness and a fulcrum for empathy.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_60591\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/03/22/agnes-martin-happiness-river-of-live/\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=680%2C683&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"683\" class=\"size-full wp-image-60591\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=240%2C241&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=320%2C321&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=768%2C771&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=600%2C603&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=32%2C32&#38;ssl=1 32w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=50%2C50&#38;ssl=1 50w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=64%2C64&#38;ssl=1 64w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=96%2C96&#38;ssl=1 96w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=128%2C128&#38;ssl=1 128w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Summer 1964</em> by Agnes Martin</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Laing writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Empathy is not something that happens to us when we read Dickens. It’s work. What art does is provide material with which to think: new registers, new spaces. After that, friend, it’s up to you. </p>\n<p>I don’t think art has a duty to be beautiful or uplifting, and some of the work I’m most drawn to refuses to traffic in either of those qualities. What I care about more&#8230; are the ways in which it’s concerned with resistance and repair.</p></blockquote>\n<p>A writer &#8212; a good writer &#8212; cannot write about art without writing about those who make it, about the lives of artists as the crucible of their creative contribution, about the delicate, triumphant art of living as a body in the world and a soul outside standard society. Olivia Laing is an excellent writer. Out of lives as varied as those of Basquiat and Agnes Martin, Derek Jarman and Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe, David Bowie and Joseph Cornell, she constructs an orrery of art as a cosmos of human connection and a sensemaking mechanism for living. </p>\n<p>In a sentiment to which I relate <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/11/01/figuring/\">in my own approach to historical lives</a>, Laing frames her method of inquiry:</p>\n<blockquote><p>I’m going as a scout, hunting for resources and ideas that might be liberating or sustaining now, and in the future. What drives all these essays is a long-standing interest in how a person can be free, and especially in how to find a freedom that is shareable, and not dependent upon the oppression or exclusion of other people.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>We’re so often told that art can’t really change anything. But I think it can. It shapes our ethical landscapes; it opens us to the interior lives of others. It is a training ground for possibility. It makes plain inequalities, and it offers other ways of living.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Throughout these short, scrumptiously insightful and sensitive essays, Laing draws on the lives of artists &#8212; the wildly uneven topographies of wildly diverse interior worlds &#8212; to contour new landscapes of possibility for life itself, as we each live it, around and through and with art. In the essay about <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/tag/georgia-okeeffe/\">Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe</a> &#8212; who revolutionized modern art <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/09/17/georgia-o-keeffe-letters-anita-pollitzer/\">while living alone and impoverished in the middle of the desert, in the middle of the world&#8217;s first global war</a> &#8212; Laing observes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>How do you make the most of what’s inside you, your talents and desires, when they slam you up against a wall of prejudice, of limiting beliefs about what a woman must be and an artist can do?</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>From the beginning, New Mexico represented salvation, though not in the wooden sense of the hill-dominating crosses she so often painted. O’Keeffe’s salvation was earthy, even pagan, comprised of the cold-water pleasure of working unceasingly at what you love, burning anxiety away beneath the desert sun.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_66261\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=680%2C1092&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"1092\" class=\"size-full wp-image-66261\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?w=800&#38;ssl=1 800w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=240%2C386&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=320%2C514&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=768%2C1234&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=600%2C964&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe, <em>Grey Lines with Black, Blue and Yellow</em>, 1923 (Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe Museum)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In an essay about another artist &#8212; the painter Chantal Joffe, for whom Laing sat &#8212; she echoes Jackson Pollock&#8217;s&#8217;s observation that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2013/04/09/jackson-pollock-selden-rodman-conversations-with-artists/\">&#8220;every good artist paints what he is,&#8221;</a> and writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>You can’t paint reality: you can only paint your own place in it, the view from your eyes, as manifested by your own hands.</p>\n<p>A painting betrays fantasies and feelings, it bestows beauty or takes it away; eventually, it supplants the body in history. A painting is full of desire and love, or greed, or hate. It radiates moods, just like people.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>Paint as fur, as velvet, as brocade, as hair. Paint as a way of entering and becoming someone else. Paint as a device for stopping time.</p></blockquote>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2013/05/03/life-doesnt-frighten-me-maya-angelou-basquiat/\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/angelousbasquiat3.jpg\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art by Basquiat from <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2013/05/03/life-doesnt-frighten-me-maya-angelou-basquiat/\"><em>Life Doesn&#8217;t Frighten Me</em></a> by Maya Angelou</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In another essay, Laing offers an exquisite counterpoint to the barbed-wire fencing off of identities that has increasingly made the free reach of human connection &#8212; that raw material and final product of all art &#8212; dangerous and damnable in a culture bristling with ready indignations and antagonisms: </p>\n<blockquote><p>A writer I was on a panel with said, and I’m paraphrasing here, that it is no longer desirable to write about the lives of other people or experiences one hasn’t had. I didn’t agree. I think writing about other people, making art about other people, is both dangerous and necessary. There are moral lines. There are limits to the known. But there’s a difference between respecting people’s right to tell or not tell their own stories and refusing to look at all.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>It depends whether you believe that we exist primarily as categories of people, who cannot communicate across our differences, or whether you think we have a common life, an obligation to regard and learn about each other.</p></blockquote>\n<p>In a sense, the entire book is a quiet manifesto for unselfing through the art we make and the art we cherish &#8212; a subtle and steadfast act of resistance to the attrition of human connection under the cultural forces of self-righteousness and sanctimonious othering, a stance against those fashionable and corrosive forces that so often indict as appropriation the mere act of learning beautiful things from each other. </p>\n<p>In another essay &#8212; about Ali Smith, the subject to whom Laing feels, or at least reads, the closest &#8212; she quotes a kindred sentiment of Smith&#8217;s:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Art is one of the prime ways we have of opening ourselves and going beyond ourselves. That’s what art is, it’s the product of the human being in the world and imagination, all coming together. The irrepressibility of the life in the works, regardless of the times, the histories, the life stories, it’s like being given the world, its darks and lights. At which point we can go about the darks and lights with our imagination energised.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72064\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=680%2C531&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"531\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72064\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?w=1566&#38;ssl=1 1566w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=240%2C187&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=320%2C250&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=768%2C599&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=600%2C468&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Joseph Cornell, <em>Untitled (Celestial Navigation)</em>, 1958. (U.S. Department of State / Joseph and Robert Cornell Memorial Foundation)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Among the subjects of a subset of essays Laing aptly categorizes as &#8220;love letters&#8221; is John Berger, whose lovely notion of &#8220;hospitality&#8221; radiates from Laing&#8217;s own work &#8212; a notion she defines as &#8220;a capacity to enlarge and open, a corrective to the overwhelming political imperative, in ascendance once again this decade, to wall off, separate and reject.&#8221; She reflects on being stopped up short by Berger&#8217;s embodiment of such hospitality when she saw him speak at the British Library at the end of his long, intellectually generous life:</p>\n<blockquote><p>It struck me then how rare it is to see a writer on stage actually thinking, and how glib and polished most speakers are. For Berger, thought was work, as taxing and rewarding as physical labour, a bringing of something real into the world. You have to strive and sweat; the act is urgent but might also fail.</p>\n<p>He talked that evening about hospitality. It was such a Bergerish notion. Hospitality: the friendly and generous reception and entertainment of guests, visitors or strangers, a word that shares its origin with hospital, a place to treat sick or injured people. This impetus towards kindness and care for the sick and strange, the vulnerable and dispossessed is everywhere in Berger’s work, the sprawling orchard of words he planted and tended over the decades.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>Art he saw as a communal and vital possession, to be written about with sensual exactness&#8230; Capitalism, he wrote in <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2012/09/28/ways-of-seeing-john-berger/\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Ways of Seeing</em></a>, survives by forcing the majority to define their own interests as narrowly as possible. It was narrowness he set himself against, the toxic impulse to wall in or wall off. Be generous to the strange, be open to difference, cross-pollinate freely. He put his faith in the people, the whole host of us.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_58043\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/03/22/agnes-martin-happiness-river-of-live/\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=680%2C693&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Agnes Martin, With My Back to the World, 1997\" width=\"680\" height=\"693\" class=\"size-full wp-image-58043\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=240%2C245&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=320%2C326&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=768%2C783&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=600%2C612&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=32%2C32&#38;ssl=1 32w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=50%2C50&#38;ssl=1 50w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=64%2C64&#38;ssl=1 64w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>With My Back to the World</em> by Agnes Martin, 1997</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In a superb 2015 essay titled &#8220;The Future of Loneliness&#8221; &#8212; an essay that bloomed into a book a year later, the <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/07/11/the-lonely-city-olivia-laing/\">splendid and unclassifiable book</a> that first enchanted me with Laing&#8217;s writing and the mind from which it springs &#8212; she considers how technology is mediating our already uneasy relationship to loneliness, and how art redeems the simulacra of belonging with which social media entrap us in this Stockholm syndrome of self-regard. In a passage of chillingly intimate resonance to all of us alive in the age of screens and selfies and the vacant, addictive affirmation of people we have never dined with tapping heart- and thumb-shaped icons on cold LED screens, she writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Loneliness centres around the act of being seen. When a person is lonely, they long to be witnessed, accepted, desired, at the same time as becoming intensely wary of exposure. According to research carried out over the past decade at the University of Chicago, the feeling of loneliness triggers what psychologists term hypervigilance for social threat. In this state, which is entered into unknowingly, the individual becomes hyperalert to rejection, becoming inclined to perceive their social interactions as tinged with hostility or scorn. The result of this shift in perception is a vicious circle of withdrawal, in which the lonely person becomes increasingly suspicious, intensifying their sense of isolation.</p>\n<p>This is where online engagement seems to exercise its special charm. Hidden behind a computer screen, the lonely person has control. They can search for company without the danger of being revealed or found wanting. They can reach out or they can hide; they can lurk and they can show themselves, safe from the humiliation of face-to-face rejection. The screen acts as a kind of protective membrane, a scrim that permits invisibility and also transformation. You can filter your image, concealing unattractive elements, and you can emerge enhanced: an online avatar designed to attract likes. But now a problem arises, for the contact this produces is not quite the same thing as intimacy. Curating a perfected self might win followers or Facebook friends, but it will not necessarily cure loneliness, since the cure for loneliness is not being looked at, but being seen and accepted as a whole person: ugly, unhappy and awkward as well as radiant and selfie-ready.</p></blockquote>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/05/27/ulysses-mimmo-paladino/\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/ulysses_paladino1.jpg?w=680&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Illustration by Mimmo Paladino for <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/05/27/ulysses-mimmo-paladino/\">a rare edition of James Joyce&#8217;s <em>Ulysses</em></a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>Having met with Ryan Trecartin &#8212; &#8220;a baby-faced thirty-four-year-old&#8221; of whom I had never heard (saying more about my odd nineteenth-century life than about his art) but whose early-twenty-first-century films about the lurid and discomposing thrill of digital culture prompted <em>The New Yorker</em> to describe him as &#8220;the most consequential artist to have emerged since the nineteen-eighties&#8221; &#8212; Laing reflects:</p>\n<blockquote><p>My own understanding of loneliness relied on a belief in solid, separate selves that he saw as hopelessly outmoded. In his world view, everyone was perpetually slipping into each other, passing through perpetual cycles of transformation; no longer separate, but interspersed. Perhaps he was right. We aren’t as solid as we once thought. We’re embodied but we’re also networks, expanding out into empty space, living on inside machines and in other people’s heads, memories and data streams as well as flesh. We’re being watched and we do not have control. We long for contact and it makes us afraid. But as long as we’re still capable of feeling and expressing vulnerability, intimacy stands a chance.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Vulnerability &#8212; which Laing unfussily terms &#8220;the necessary condition of love&#8221; &#8212; is indeed the bellowing undertone of these essays: vulnerability as frisson and function of art, of life itself, of the atavistic impulse for transmuting living into meaning that we call art. </p>\n<p>Complement the thoroughly symphonic <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Funny-Weather-Emergency-Olivia-Laing/dp/132400570X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=_\"blank\"><strong><em>Funny Weather</em></strong></a> with Paul Klee on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/09/24/paul-klee-tree-artist-creativity/\">creativity and why an artist is like a tree</a>, Kafka on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/07/03/kafka-music-art/\">why we make art</a>, Egon Schiele on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/06/01/egon-schiele-letter/\">why visionary artists tend to come from the minority</a>, and Virginia Woolf&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/09/09/virginia-woolf-cotton-wool-moments-of-being/\">garden epiphany about what it means to be an artist</a> &#8212; which remains, for me, the single most beautiful and penetrating thing ever written on the subject &#8212; then revisit Laing on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/05/16/olivia-laing-to-the-river/\">life, loss, and the wisdom of rivers</a>.  </p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter. Popova","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/funnyweather_laing.jpg?fit=320%2C486&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn21@2020_3/2021/04/03/04-49-36-461_8c825741f4e7870a.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/georgiaokeeffe3.jpg?w=680&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn48@2020_2/2021/04/03/04-49-44-357_ecc6724b0bf9a647.webp","https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/agnesmartin_summer1964.jpg?resize=680%2C683&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn86@2020_2/2021/04/03/04-49-42-288_6dae329e5b7353f3.webp","https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/11/GeorgiaOkeeffe1925.jpg?resize=680%2C1092&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn79@2020_4/2021/04/03/04-49-44-666_0c073e9114167048.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2013/05/angelousbasquiat3.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn100@2020_4/2021/04/03/04-49-44-826_db8037df689efbae.webp","https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/11/josephcornell1.jpg?resize=680%2C531&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn60@2020_6/2021/04/03/04-49-46-109_6ec381cca7efe390.webp","https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/10/agnesmartin_withmybacktotheworld.jpg?resize=680%2C693&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn99@2020_1/2021/04/03/04-49-42-251_4202b229284cf9b7.webp","https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/ulysses_paladino1.jpg?w=680&ssl=1":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn95@2020_5/2021/04/03/04-49-46-170_376b79654523b731.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/brainpickings/rss/~4/4HxTf__B_4c":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn67@2020_2/2021/04/03/04-49-46-745_8a43547519357ae3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1617425326522},{"createdTime":"2021-04-03 12:48:46","updatedTime":"2021-04-03 12:48:46","title":"How Pythagoras and Sappho Radicalized Music and Revolutionized the World","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=72835","description":"<h3>The story of the invention of the love song, the world&#8217;s first algorithm, and the mathematics of transcendence. </h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Subversive-History-Ted-Gioia/dp/1541644360/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"495\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/tedgoia_musicsubversive.jpg?fit=320%2C495&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"How Pythagoras and Sappho Radicalized Music and Revolutionized the World\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/tedgoia_musicsubversive.jpg?w=646&#38;ssl=1 646w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/tedgoia_musicsubversive.jpg?resize=240%2C372&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/tedgoia_musicsubversive.jpg?resize=320%2C495&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/tedgoia_musicsubversive.jpg?resize=600%2C929&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>“To create today is to create dangerously,&#8221; Albert Camus <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/05/02/albert-camus-create-dangerously/\">told a gathering of young people</a> at the peak of the Cold War, shortly after becoming the second-youngest laureate of the Nobel Prize. &#8220;The question, for all those who cannot live without art and what it signifies, is merely to find out how, among the police forces of so many ideologies&#8230; the strange liberty of creation is possible.” A generation before him, while policed by the forces about to unworld humanity in its first global war, the revolutionary artist Egon Schiele observed that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/06/01/egon-schiele-letter/\">true visionaries tend to come from the minority</a>.</p>\n<p>Millennia and civilizations earlier, two such visionaries who lived a generation apart, one born the day the other threw herself into the sea &#8212; <strong>Sappho</strong> (c. 630&#8211;c. 570 BC) and <strong>Pythagoras</strong> (c. 570&#8211;c. 495 BC) &#8212; revolutionized the deepest undertone of modern thought with their repugnant ideas about the most delicate, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/03/15/writers-on-music/\">most beloved</a>, and most elemental of the arts: music.  </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_64713\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/pythagoras_knapp.jpg?resize=676%2C1024&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"676\" height=\"1024\" class=\"size-full wp-image-64713\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/pythagoras_knapp.jpg?w=676&#38;ssl=1 676w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/pythagoras_knapp.jpg?resize=240%2C364&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/pythagoras_knapp.jpg?resize=320%2C485&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/pythagoras_knapp.jpg?resize=600%2C909&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 676px) 100vw, 676px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Pythagoras (Art by J. Augustus Knapp, circa 1926)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In an era when the most widespread musical instrument was the tetrachord &#8212; the Hellenic four-string lyre &#8212; and musicians had no standardized system of tuning their instruments, no understanding of the underlying tonal patterns, and nothing more than a vague intuitive sense about how to strum melodies rather than discord, Pythagoras discovered the relationship between musical harmony and the mathematical harmony of numbers. According to his foremost biographer, the fourth-century Syrian scholar Iamblichus, Pythagoras took it upon himself to devise a mechanical aid for musical tuning. </p>\n<p>One day, Iamblichus’s account goes, Pythagoras was strolling past a blacksmith’s forge and was captivated by the sound of the many hammers pounding in a pattern that suddenly sounded harmonious. He rushed into the forge and immediately began investigating the cause of the harmony, testing the various hammers in various stroke combinations &#8212; some producing harmony, others discord. After analyzing the patterns and weighing the hammers, he discovered a simple mathematical relationship between those that produced harmony &#8212; their masses were exact ratios of one another’s. </p>\n<p>Although the anecdote may belong to that murky shoreline between the apocryphal and the factual that marks many biographies of genius, Pythagoras did eventually test these ratios on the lyre. They proved to be perfectly predictive of harmony &#8212; the first discovery of a mathematical rule undergirding a physical phenomenon, and the basis of what became known as the Music of the Spheres.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72847\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?resize=680%2C245&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"245\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72847\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?resize=240%2C87&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?resize=320%2C115&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?resize=768%2C277&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/harmonicesmundi_kepler.jpg?resize=600%2C217&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Celestial harmonics of the planets, from <em>The Harmony of the World</em> (1619) by Johannes Kepler, based on the Pythagorean concept of the Music of the Spheres.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In our time, Pythagoras, known to every schoolchild for his famous triangle theorem, is celebrated as the pioneer who set the golden age of mathematics into motion with the development of numeric logic. Having coined the word <em>philosophy</em> and <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/05/23/pythagoras-olympic-games/\">defined the very meaning of wisdom</a>, he seeded scientific ideas that fomented the later revolutions ushered in by visionaries as far-ranging and far-reaching as Plato, Copernicus, Descartes, Kepler, Newton, and Einstein. </p>\n<p>But in his time, Pythagoras was very much a radical, a dissident, an intellectual deviant. His progressive views on social reform led him to flee the tyrannical rule of his native Samos. After arriving in the Greek colony of Croton as a refugee, he founded a philosophical school whose disciples, known as the Pythagoreans, devised an unexampled model of the universe, placing at its center a ball of fire more than a thousand years before Copernicus upended the geocentric Ptolemaic system with his heliocentricity. </p>\n<p>In another stroke of radicalism, the Pythagoreans admitted into their school a class of sub-citizens denied education and excluded from the newborn civic system of democracy: women. One of them became the world’s first known female astronomer &#8212; Hypatia, who lived her trailblazing life and died her savage death in the city where nearly every trace of Sappho vanished.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72839\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/death-of-sappho-by-miguel-carbonell-selva-1881_print?sku=s6-19311298p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?resize=680%2C989&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"989\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-72843\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?resize=240%2C349&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?resize=320%2C465&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?resize=768%2C1117&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/deathofsappho_selva.jpg?resize=600%2C873&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Death of Sappho</em> by Miguel Carbonell Selva, 1881. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/death-of-sappho-by-miguel-carbonell-selva-1881_print?sku=s6-19311298p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\">as a print</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>When the Library of Alexandria was burned, the flames consumed the nine-volume set of Sappho&#8217;s collected works, leaving only fragments copied by fans and scholars throughout the ancient world. From this handful of surviving ashes, Sappho rose with her lyre and her verse to be remembered as the Tenth Muse, the inventor of the love song and the personal lyric, the first great beacon of women’s right to creative expression, and the first great champion of the right to love whom we love. Unlike Emily Dickinson, who deliberately changed the gender pronouns in her poems to conceal <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/12/10/emily-dickinson-love-letters-susan-gilbert/\">the same-sex passion</a> that fomented her poetry, Sappho kept the female pronouns in the <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/09/29/sappho-i-simply-want-to-be-dead-constellation-chor/\">beautiful and heartbreaking odes</a> she wrote to the women she loved. In doing so, she pioneered a radical shift in musical culture &#8212; the permission to sing not about the gods, the seasons, and the wars, but about oneself: about the stunning interior universe of subjective human experience. Without Sappho, there would be no Nina Simone to pose in song <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/05/25/christof-koch-consciousness-qualia/\">the central question of consciousness</a>: “I wish you could know what it means to be me.”</p>\n<p>The epoch-making contributions of Pythagoras and Sappho come alive in Ted Gioia&#8217;s altogether wonderful book <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Subversive-History-Ted-Gioia/dp/1541644360/?tag=braipick-20\" target=_\"blank\"><strong><em>Music: A Subversive History</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/music-a-subversive-history/oclc/1184234686&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\"><em>public library</em></a>) &#8212; the story of our species told through its most consummate and intimate art-form, traced through the lives of the visionaries and radicals who shaped it, from Pythagoras and Sappho to Bob Dylan and N.W.A. </p>\n<p>In a sentiment that calls to mind Iris Murdoch&#8217;s astute observation that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/02/12/iris-murdoch-salvation-by-words/\">&#8220;tyrants always fear art because tyrants want to mystify while art tends to clarify,&#8221;</a> Gioia frames his approach:</p>\n<blockquote><p>At every stage in human history, music has been a catalyst for change, challenging conventions and conveying coded messages &#8212; or, not infrequently, delivering blunt, unambiguous ones. It has given voice to individuals and groups denied access to other platforms for expression, so much so that, in many times and places, freedom of song has been as important as freedom of speech, and far more controversial.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72851\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/science-and-literature-in-the-middle-ages-1878_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?resize=680%2C800&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"800\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72851\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?w=1348&#38;ssl=1 1348w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?resize=240%2C282&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?resize=320%2C376&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?resize=768%2C904&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/pythagoras.jpg?resize=600%2C706&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art from an 1878 book about the history of science and literature, depicting some of Pythagoras&#8217;s influences and inspirations. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/science-and-literature-in-the-middle-ages-1878_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\">as a print</a> and <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\">as a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Holding up Pythagoras as the most significant figure in the history of music, whose controversial contribution was both a liberation and a limitation, Gioia writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Greek culture before his arrival revered what we call nowadays Orphic thought (named after Orpheus, the mythical musician, but almost certainly considered a historical personage in those distant days), and believed songs possessed powerful magic. The rise of Pythagorean music theory, circa 500 BC, changed all that by conceptualizing music as a rational science of sounds that could be described in mathematical terms.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Looking back to antiquity from our own era, in which <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/01/24/nick-cave-music-ai/\">algorithms are mediating the relationship between music, feeling, and transcendence</a>, Gioia adds:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The very first algorithm entered Western music with this philosophical rupture that happened more than 2,500 years ago.</p></blockquote>\n<p>And yet while the Pythagorean model of mathematically distinguishing melody from noise liberated music by giving it a new language of codified expression, it also limited music by excluding from the musical canon styles that didn&#8217;t conform to these proportionate structures of scales and rhythms &#8212; styles like those that emerged from the African diaspora or from my own native Balkans. Gioia writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The ratios and proportions that initially helped us grasp songs turned into the rules and constraints that defined them. The strategies and schemas were often seen as the ‘authentic’ music, and the actual sounds only got validation through their allegiance to what was written on the printed page&#8230; The eventual result was a conceptualization of music that excluded far more than it allowed.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>The very practice of legitimization is an act of distortion.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Still, the Pythagorean conceptualization of music had profound and beneficent consequences, stretching far beyond the realm of music and into the entire landscape of culture: By bringing mathematics to an art-form <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/06/28/an-essay-toward-a-natural-history-of-serpents/\">previously regarded as mystical</a>, it catalyzed the slow shift from a world of superstition and magic to a world of science and reason &#8212; a cultural evolution that would unfold on the scale of epochs. Two millennia after Pythagoras, Kepler would spend years <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/12/26/katharina-kepler-witchcraft-dream/\">defending his mother in a witchcraft trial</a> while revolutionizing our understanding of the universe with his epoch-making laws of planetary motion, drawing on the Music of the Spheres to discover the proportional relationships of planetary orbits. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72848\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-the-harmony-of-the-world-by-johannes-kepler-1619_print?sku=s6-19311111p4a1v46?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?resize=680%2C768&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"768\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72848\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?resize=240%2C271&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?resize=320%2C361&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?resize=768%2C867&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/kepler_solids.jpg?resize=600%2C678&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Solids from Kepler&#8217;s <em>Harmony of the World</em>, exploring the relationship between harmony and geometry. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-the-harmony-of-the-world-by-johannes-kepler-1619_print?sku=s6-19311111p4a1v46?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\">as a print</a> and <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\">as a face mask</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>From this foundation laid in the Pythagorean past, Gioia leaps across the millennia to the present:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Today, these three spheres &#8212; science, music, and magic &#8212; appear as self-contained and unrelated disciplines, but in the context of 500 BC, the connections between them were obvious to the leading minds. Anyone who hoped to dislodge magical thinking in a traditional society and replace it with a scientific worldview was forced to address music theory, because it, too, could be conceptualized as either magic or science. Any choice between these two models would have profound implications. And not just for theory: society would be altered by how this matter was decided. Before Pythagoras, songs possessed magical potency. If Pythagoras and his followers hoped to eradicate superstition and elevate a more rational and logical worldview, they were almost forced to redefine all the parameters of musical practice.</p></blockquote>\n<p>But while this reconfiguration of musical practice as a mathematical language advanced the world toward science, it also repressed a central animating force of music &#8212; its elemental humanity, ablaze with feeling, sensuality, and a sense of the sacred. Even Schopenhauer, so very German and so unfaltering in his central tenet of the will as the supreme instrument of the human spirit, considered music capable of reaching beyond the reach of will, into <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/04/28/schopenhauer-on-the-power-of-music/\">&#8220;the inner being, the in-itself, of the world&#8221;</a>; even Kierkegaard, for all his ceaseless cerebration, his Nordic reserve, and his lifelong virginity, exulted in <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/05/05/kierkegaard-mozart-music/\">the unparalleled sensuality of music</a>. </p>\n<p>It was Sappho who feathered the other great wing by which music took flight toward modernity, carrying the whole of human culture on its back. Gioia writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Sappho has two obvious concerns, and they dominate her worldview even as they expose a hidden rift in Western thought: the emotional bonds of love, and communal obligations to the gods. In the later evolution of Western music, these two approaches will veer off into their separate traditions and have little to do with each other. You could hardly imagine two music genres with less in common than love songs and religious hymns, but for Sappho these are intimately connected.</p></blockquote>\n<p>While Pythagoras took the mysticism of music and turned it into a mathematical language, Sappho took the ancient tradition of sacred singing and turned it into a new literary genre of personal poetics. By pioneering the love song and the self-permission for telling our own stories, drawn from our most intimate experiences, she gave the world an immense and abiding gift &#8212; the ability to preserve our stories in song as a fundament of identity and survival, from the African spirituals that sustained the souls of the enslaved to the folk ballads by which refugees hold on to culture and community. In fact, Sappho&#8217;s home island has always been a nexus of cultures and remains a major portal into Europe for refugees from the Middle East. Gioia writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>At the height of the Syrian crisis in 2016, new arrivals would show up on Lesbos almost every day, making their treacherous journey on small boats, rafts, and inflatable crafts&#8230; Songs are the possessions most likely to survive long journeys, remaining the property of the newcomer even when everything else has been taken away.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Complement this fragment of the altogether fascinating <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Subversive-History-Ted-Gioia/dp/1541644360/?tag=braipick-20\" target=_\"blank\"><strong><em>Music: A Subversive History</em></strong></a> with a haunting choral invocation of <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/09/29/sappho-i-simply-want-to-be-dead-constellation-chor/\">Sappho&#8217;s timeless elegy for heartbreak</a>, then revisit Maurice Sendak on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/10/17/the-shape-of-music-maurice-sendak/\">the shape of music as the key to storytelling</a> and some of humanity&#8217;s most beloved storytellers on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/03/15/writers-on-music/\">the singular power of music</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter. 12:48:46","updatedTime":"2021-04-03 12:48:46","title":"Dorothy Lathrop’s Dreamscapes: Haunting Century-Old Illustrations of Fairy-Poems by the Woman Who Became the First to Win the Caldecott Medal","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=72910","description":"<h3>Poetic enchantments in pen, ink, and imagination.</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Down-Adown-Derry-Walter-Mare/dp/1434472256/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"480\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?fit=320%2C480&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"Dorothy Lathrop&#8217;s Dreamscapes: Haunting Century-Old Illustrations of Fairy-Poems by the Woman Who Became the First to Win the Caldecott Medal\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?w=900&#38;ssl=1 900w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?resize=320%2C480&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?resize=600%2C900&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?resize=240%2C360&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop.jpg?resize=768%2C1152&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>In the final stretch of World War I, having earned a degree from the Pennsylvania Academy of Fine Arts in an era when under 4% of women graduated college, <strong>Dorothy Pulis Lathrop</strong> (April 16, 1891&#8211;December 30, 1980) was commissioned to illustrate a book of experimental imagist poems by a young American poet who would win the Pulitzer Prize two decades later. She never received her commission &#8212; the publisher went bankrupt. But her art captured the attentive imagination of the prolific English poet and novelist Walter de la Mare, who asked her to illustrate a fairy tale he had written a decade earlier. So began a lifelong friendship and Lathrop&#8217;s own prolific career. In her nine decades of life, she illustrated nearly forty books for children and wrote several, most of them celebrating her love of animals and the subtle beauties of the natural world. She become the first person to win the Caldecott Medal &#8212; the Nobel Prize of illustration.</p>\n<p>In 1922, Lathrop illustrated De la Mare&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Down-Adown-Derry-Walter-Mare/dp/1434472256/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Down-Adown-Derry</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/down-adown-derry-a-book-of-fairy-poems-with-illus-by-dorothy-p-lathrop/oclc/976959051&#38;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a> | <a href=\"https://archive.org/details/downadownderrybo00delauoft/page/15/mode/2up\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public domain</em></a>) &#8212; a collection of fairy poems for children, to which she brought her uncommon imagination and her delicate, scrumptiously detailed pen.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72916\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-by-dorothy-lathrop-for-down-adown-derry-by-walter-de-la-mare-19224642772_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?resize=680%2C801&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"801\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72916\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop17.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, print</a>.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>While the artwork radiates the aesthetic of the Golden Age of Illustration that Arthur Rackham had ushered in fifteen years earlier with his <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/02/01/arthur-rackham-alice-in-wonderland/\">revolutionary edition of <em>Alice&#8217;s Adventures in Wonderland</em></a>, it also radiates Lathrop&#8217;s singular sensibility &#8212; lines as consummate as Rackham&#8217;s, but with a greater and more dreamlike elegance of movement; stunning dramas in black and white, evocative of <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/01/25/aubrey-beardsley-oscar-wilde-salome/\">Aubrey Beardsley</a> but unfolding with greater subtlety and tenderness.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72929\" what kindness really is you must lose things, feel the future dissolve in a moment like salt in a weakened broth.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/100-Poems-Break-Your-Heart/dp/0544931882/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"480\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?fit=320%2C480&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"Naomi Shihab Nye&#8217;s Beloved Ode to Kindness, Animated\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?w=800&#38;ssl=1 800w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?resize=320%2C480&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?resize=600%2C899&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?resize=240%2C360&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/04/poemsbreakyourheart_hirsch.jpg?resize=768%2C1151&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>“Nothing can make our life, or the lives of other people, more beautiful than perpetual kindness,” Leo Tolstoy &#8212; a man of colossal compassion and colossal blind spots &#8212; wrote while <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/07/21/leo-tolstoy-kindness-calendar-of-wisdom/\">reckoning with his life</a> as it neared its end. </p>\n<p>“Practice kindness all day to everybody and you will realize you’re already in heaven now,” Jack Kerouac half-resolved, half-instructed an epoch later in a <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/03/12/jack-kerouac-golden-eternity/\">beautiful letter</a> to his first wife and lifelong friend.</p>\n<p>Of course, even the best-intentioned of us are not capable of perpetual kindness, not capable of being our most elevated selves all day with everybody. If you have not watched yourself, helpless and horrified, transform into an ill-tempered child with a loved one or the unsuspecting man blocking the produce aisle with his basket of bok choy, you have not lived. Discontinuous and self-contradictory even under the safest and sanest of circumstances, human beings are not wired for constancy of feeling, of conduct, of selfhood. When the world grows unsafe, when life charges at us with its stresses and its sorrows, our devotion to kindness can short-circuit with alarming ease. And yet, paradoxically, it is often in the laboratory of loss and uncertainty that we calibrate and supercharge our capacity for kindness. And it is always, as Kerouac intuited, a practice. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72914\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-by-dorothy-lathrop-for-down-adown-derry-by-walter-de-la-mare-19224642738_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?resize=680%2C979&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"979\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72914\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?w=1000&#38;ssl=1 1000w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?resize=320%2C461&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?resize=600%2C864&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?resize=240%2C346&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/downadownderry_dorothylathrop6.jpg?resize=768%2C1106&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art by Dorothy Lathrop from her <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/03/09/dorothy-lathrop-down-adown-derry/\">1922 fairy-poems</a>. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-by-dorothy-lathrop-for-down-adown-derry-by-walter-de-la-mare-19224642738_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In 1978, drawing on a <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/11/10/naomi-shihab-nye-kindness/\">jarring real-life experience</a>, <strong>Naomi Shihab Nye</strong> captured this difficult, beautiful, redemptive transmutation of fear into kindness in a poem of uncommon soulfulness and empathic wingspan that has since become a classic &#8212; a classic now part of Edward Hirsch&#8217;s finely curated anthology <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/100-Poems-Break-Your-Heart/dp/0544931882/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>100 Poems to Break Your Heart</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/100-poems-to-break-your-heart/oclc/1242943669&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>); a classic reimagined in a lovely short film by illustrator <a href=\"https://www.anaperezlopez.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Ana Pérez López</a> and my friends at the <a href=\"http://onbeing.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>On Being</em> Project</a>:</p>\n<p><iframe loading=\"lazy\" title=\"Kindness - by Naomi Shihab Nye\" src=\"https://player.vimeo.com/video/433736862?dnt=1&#38;app_id=122963\" width=\"680\" height=\"383\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"autoplay; fullscreen; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>KINDNESS</strong><br />\n<em>by Naomi Shihab Nye</em></p>\n<p>Before you know what kindness really is<br />\nyou must lose things,<br />\nfeel the future dissolve in a moment<br />\nlike salt in a weakened broth.<br />\nWhat you held in your hand,<br />\nwhat you counted and carefully saved,<br />\nall this must go so you know<br />\nhow desolate the landscape can be<br />\nbetween the regions of kindness.<br />\nHow you ride and ride<br />\nthinking the bus will never stop,<br />\nthe passengers eating maize and chicken<br />\nwill stare out the window forever.</p>\n<p>Before you learn the tender gravity of kindness,<br />\nyou must travel where the Indian in a white poncho<br />\nlies dead by the side of the road.<br />\nYou must see how this could be you,<br />\nhow he too was someone<br />\nwho journeyed through the night with plans<br />\nand the simple breath that kept him alive.</p>\n<p>Before you know kindness as the deepest thing inside,<br />\nyou must know sorrow as the other deepest thing.<br />\nYou must wake up with sorrow.<br />\nYou must speak to it till your voice<br />\ncatches the thread of all sorrows<br />\nand you see the size of the cloth.</p>\n<p>Then it is only kindness that makes sense anymore,<br />\nonly kindness that ties your shoes<br />\nand sends you out into the day to mail letters and purchase bread,<br />\nonly kindness that raises its head<br />\nfrom the crowd of the world to say<br />\nIt is I you have been looking for,<br />\nand then goes with you everywhere<br />\nlike a shadow or a friend.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Complement with a fascinating cultural history of <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/07/13/on-kindness-adam-phillips-barbara-taylor/\">how kindness became our forbidden pleasure</a>, Jacqueline Woodson&#8217;s letter to children about <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/04/15/jacqueline-woodson-velocity-of-being/\">how we learn kindness</a>, and George Sand&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/11/27/george-sand-the-mysterious-tale-of-gentle-jack-and-lord-bumblebee/\">only children&#8217;s book</a> &#8212; a poignant parable about choosing kindness and generosity over cynicism and fear &#8212; then revisit other soul-broadening animated poems: <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/04/23/singularity-marie-howe-animated/\">&#8220;Singularity&#8221;</a> by Marie Howe, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/01/29/murmuration-linda-france/\">&#8220;Murmuration&#8221;</a> by Linda France, and <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/03/12/wendell-berry-the-peace-of-wild-things-animated/\">&#8220;The Peace of Wild Things&#8221;</a> by Wendell Berry.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter. Animated","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=72939","description":"<h3>On where to seek refuge from the forethought of grief.</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Peace-Wild-Things-Other-Poems/dp/014198712X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"491\" Things: Wendell Berry&#8217;s Poetic Antidote to Despair, Animated\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/wendellberry_thepeaceofwildthings.jpg?w=1524&#38;ssl=1 1524w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/wendellberry_thepeaceofwildthings.jpg?resize=240%2C368&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/wendellberry_thepeaceofwildthings.jpg?resize=768%2C1179&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/wendellberry_thepeaceofwildthings.jpg?resize=1001%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 1001w, hundred years ago, in a prophetic book envisioning a twenty-first-century world savaged by a deadly pandemic, Mary Shelley considered <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/04/16/mary-shelley-the-last-man/\">what makes life worth living</a>, insisting that in the midst of widespread death and despair, we must seek peace in the &#8220;murmur of streams, and the gracious waving of trees, the beauteous vesture of earth, and sublime pageantry of the skies.&#8221; A century later, Willa Cather &#8212; another immensely talented, immensely underappreciated novelist and poet laureate of the human spirit &#8212; contemplated <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/08/26/willa-cather-my-antonia-happiness/\">the deepest wellspring of happiness</a> and located it in those moments when, immersed in nature, we find ourselves &#8220;dissolved into something complete and great&#8221;</a> &#8212; a line now emblazoned on Cather&#8217;s tombstone by her partner. </p>\n<p>In another half-century, <strong>Wendell Berry</strong> (b. August 5, 1934) &#8212; one of the great poets and wisest elders of our time &#8212; arrived at this elemental truth, a truth we so easily lose sight of in those times of despair when we most need it, articulating it with his uncommon tenderness and clarity of vision in the title poem of his 1968 collection <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Peace-Wild-Things-Other-Poems/dp/014198712X/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>The Peace of Wild Things</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/peace-of-wild-things/oclc/24389439&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>), composed under a thick cultural cloudscape of despair &#8212; at the peak of the Cold War and the Vietnam War, after the successive assassinations of John F. Kennedy and Dr. King, in the wake of <em>Silent Spring</em> and its <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/01/27/rachel-carson-silent-spring-dorothy-freeman/\">disquieting wakeup call</a> for our broken relationship with nature.</p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/01/14/the-blue-hour-isabelle-simler/\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/thebluehour_simler5.jpg\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art from <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/01/14/the-blue-hour-isabelle-simler/\"><em>The Blue Hour</em></a> by Isabelle Simler</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Berry &#8212; a rare seer into those subterranean landscapes of being where nature meets human nature and a rare voice of our collective ecological conscience &#8212; reads the poem in this breathtaking short film, produced by <a href=\"https://onbeing.org\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>On Being</em></a> and illustrated by English artist <a href=\"https://charlotteager.co.uk\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Charlotte Ager</a>.</p>\n<p><iframe loading=\"lazy\" title=\"“The Peace of Wild Things” by Wendell Berry, A Poetry Film by Charlotte Ager &#38; Katy Wang\" width=\"680\" height=\"383\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/-ewB0WL3bNw?feature=oembed&#38;rel=0&#38;controls=0&#38;showinfo=0&#38;iv_load_policy=3\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<blockquote><p><strong>THE PEACE OF WILD THINGS</strong><br />\n<em>by Wendell Berry</em></p>\n<p>When despair for the world grows in me<br />\nand I wake in the night at the least sound<br />\nin fear of what my life and my children’s lives may be,<br />\nI go and lie down where the wood drake<br />\nrests in his beauty on the water, and the great heron feeds.<br />\nI come into the peace of wild things<br />\nwho do not tax their lives with forethought<br />\nof grief. I come into the presence of still water.<br />\nAnd I feel above me the day-blind stars<br />\nwaiting with their light. For a time<br />\nI rest in the grace of the world, and am free.</p></blockquote>\n<p>At the last <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/the-universe-in-verse/\"><em>Universe in Verse</em></a> &#8212; my charitable celebration of science and the natural world through poetry &#8212; <em>On Being</em> creator and host <strong>Krista Tippett</strong> read Berry&#8217;s poem with a lovely prefatory meditation on how poetry gives us the language to remember our creaturely nature, which in turn reroots us in the larger web of belonging as &#8220;creatures among creatures&#8221;:</p>\n<p><iframe loading=\"lazy\" title=\"Krista Tippett Reads &#38;quot;The Peace of Wild Things&#38;quot; by Wendell Berry\" src=\"https://player.vimeo.com/video/522959267?dnt=1&#38;app_id=122963\" width=\"680\" height=\"383\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"autoplay; fullscreen; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p>Complement with Wendell Berry on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/11/17/wendell-berry-hidden-wound/\">delight as a force of resistance</a>, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/05/12/how-to-be-a-poet-wendell-berry/\">how to be a poet and a complete human being</a>, his conscience-clarifying poem <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/08/14/wendell-berry-questionnaire-amanda-palmer/\">&#8220;Questionnaire,&#8221;</a> and Krista&#8217;s soul-salving <a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/wendell-berry-ellen-davis-the-art-of-being-creatures/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>OnBeing</em> conversation</a> with him, then revisit two kindred-spirited animated poems: Marie Howe&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/04/23/singularity-marie-howe-animated/\">&#8220;Singularity&#8221;</a> and Linda France&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/01/29/murmuration-linda-france/\">&#8220;Murmuration.&#8221;</a></p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going.
